the tenant of wildfell hall author s preface to the second edition while i acknowledge the success of the present work to have be great than i anticipate and the praise it have elicit from a few kind critic to have be great than it deserve i must also admit that from some other quarter it have be censure with an asperity which i be as little prepared to expect and which my judgment as well as my feeling assure i be more bitter than just
it be scarcely the province of an author to refute the argument of his censor and vindicate his own production but i may be allow to make here a few observation with which i would have preface the first edition have i foresee the necessity of such precaution against the misapprehension of those who would read it with a prejudiced mind or be content to judge it by a hasty glance
my object in write the follow page be not simply to amuse the reader neither be it to gratify my own taste nor yet to ingratiate myself with the press and the public i wish to tell the truth for truth always convey its own moral to those who be able to receive it
but as the priceless treasure too frequently hide at the bottom of a well it need some courage to dive for it especially as he that do so will be likely to incur more scorn and obloquy for the mud and water into which he have venture to plunge than thank for the jewel he procure as in like manner she who undertake the cleansing of a careless bachelor s apartment will be liable to more abuse for the dust she raise than commendation for the clearance she effect
let it not be imagine however that i consider myself competent to reform the error and abuse of society but only that i would fain contribute my humble quota towards so good an aim and if i can gain the public ear at all i would rather whisper a few wholesome truth therein than much soft nonsense
as the story of agnes grey be accuse of extravagant over- colour in those very part that be carefully copy from the life with a most scrupulous avoidance of all exaggeration so in the present work i find myself censure for depict con amore with a morbid love of the coarse if not of the brutal those scene which i will venture to say have not be more painful for the most fastidious of my critic to read than they be for i to describe
i may have go too far in which case i shall be careful not to trouble myself or my reader in the same way again but when we have to do with vice and vicious character i maintain it be well to depict they as they really be than as they would wish to appear
to represent a bad thing in its least offensive light be doubtless the most agreeable course for a writer of fiction to pursue but be it the most honest or the safe
be it well to reveal the snare and pitfall of life to the young and thoughtless traveller or to cover they with branch and flower
oh reader
if there be less of this delicate concealment of fact this whispering peace peace when there be no peace there would be less of sin and misery to the young of both sex who be leave to wring their bitter knowledge from experience
i would not be understand to suppose that the proceeding of the unhappy scapegrace with his few profligate companion i have here introduce be a speciman of the common practice of society the case be an extreme one as i trust none would fail to perceive but i know that such character do exist and if i have warn one rash youth from follow in their step or prevent one thoughtless girl from fall into the very natural error of my heroine the book have not be write in vain
but at the same time if any honest reader shall have derive more pain than pleasure from its perusal and have close the last volume with a disagreeable impression on his mind i humbly crave his pardon for such be far from my intention and i will endeavour to do well another time for i love to give innocent pleasure
yet be it understand i shall not limit my ambition to this or even to produce a perfect work of art time and talent so spend i should consider waste and misapply
such humble talent as god have give i i will endeavour to put to their great use if i be able to amuse i will try to benefit too and when i feel it my duty to speak an unpalatable truth with the help of god i will speak it though it be to the prejudice of my name and to the detriment of my reader s immediate pleasure as well as my own
one word more and i have do
respect the author s identity i would have it to he distinctly understand that acton bell be neither currer nor ellis bell and therefore let not his fault be attribute to they
as to whether the name be real or fictitious it cannot greatly signify to those who know he only by his work
as little i should think can it matter whether the writer so designate be a man or a woman as one or two of my critic profess to have discover
i take the imputation in good part as a compliment to the just delineation of my female character and though i be bind to attribute much of the severity of my censor to this suspicion i make no effort to refute it because in my own mind i be satisfied that if a book be a good one it be so whatever the sex of the author may be
all novel be or should be write for both man and woman to read and i be at a loss to conceive how a man should permit himself to write anything that would be really disgraceful to a woman or why a woman should be censure for write anything that would be proper and become for a man
july 22 nd 1848
the tenant of wildfell hall chapter i you must go back with i to the autumn of 1827
my father as you know be a sort of gentleman farmer in shire and i by his express desire succeed he in the same quiet occupation not very willingly for ambition urge i to high aim and self-conceit assure i that in disregard its voice i be bury my talent in the earth and hide my light under a bushel
my mother have do her utmost to persuade i that i be capable of great achievement but my father who think ambition be the sure road to ruin and change but another word for destruction would listen to no scheme for better either my own condition or that of my fellow mortal
he assure i it be all rubbish and exhort i with his die breath to continue in the good old way to follow his step and those of his father before he and let my high ambition be to walk honestly through the world look neither to the right hand nor to the left and to transmit the paternal acre to my child in at least as flourish a condition as he leave they to i
well
an honest and industrious farmer be one of the most useful member of society and if i devote my talent to the cultivation of my farm and the improvement of agriculture in general i shall thereby benefit not only my own immediate connection and dependant but in some degree mankind at large hence i shall not have live in vain with such reflection as these i be endeavour to console myself as i plod home from the field one cold damp cloudy evening towards the close of october
but the gleam of a bright red fire through the parlour window have more effect in cheer my spirit and rebuke my thankless repining than all the sage reflection and good resolution i have force my mind to frame for i be young then remember only four-and- twenty and have not acquire half the rule over my own spirit that i now possess trifling as that may be
however that haven of bliss must not be enter till i have exchange my miry boot for a clean pair of shoe and my rough surtout for a respectable coat and make myself generally presentable before decent society for my mother with all her kindness be vastly particular on certain point
in ascend to my room i be meet upon the stair by a smart pretty girl of nineteen with a tidy dumpy figure a round face bright bloom cheek glossy cluster curl and little merry brown eye
i need not tell you this be my sister rose
she be i know a comely matron still and doubtless no less lovely in your eye than on the happy day you first behold she
nothing tell i then that she a few year hence would be the wife of one entirely unknown to i as yet but destine hereafter to become a close friend than even herself more intimate than that unmannerly lad of seventeen by whom i be collar in the passage on come down and well-nigh jerk off my equilibrium and who in correction for his impudence receive a resounding whack over the sconce which however sustain no serious injury from the infliction as besides be more than commonly thick it be protect by a redundant shock of short reddish curl that my mother call auburn

and fergus what have you be do badger-baiting and here he proceed to give a particular account of his sport and the respective trait of prowess evince by the badger and the dog my mother pretend to listen with deep attention and watch his animate countenance with a degree of maternal admiration i think highly disproportione to its object

as for i i be hungry and content myself with silently demolish the tea ham and toast while my mother and sister go on talk and continue to discuss the apparent or non- apparent circumstance and probable or improbable history of the mysterious lady but i must confess that after my brother s misadventure i once or twice raise the cup to my lip and put it down again without dare to taste the content lest i should injure my dignity by a similar explosion

now halford before i close this letter iwill tell you who eliza millward be she be the vicar s young daughter and a very engage little creature for whom i feel no small degree of partiality and she know it though i have never come to any direct explanation and have no definite intention of so do for my mother who maintain there be no one good enough for i within twenty mile round could not bear the thought of my marrying that insignificant little thing who in addition to she numerous other disqualification have not twenty pound to call she own
eliza s figure be at once slight and plump her face small and nearly as round as my sister s complexion something similar to her but more delicate and less decidedly bloom nose retrousse feature generally irregular and altogether she be rather charming than pretty
but her eye i must not forget those remarkable feature for therein her chief attraction lay in outward aspect at least they be long and narrow in shape the irid black or very dark brown the expression various and ever change but always either preternaturally i have almost say diabolically wicked or irresistibly bewitch often both
her voice be gentle and childish her tread light and soft as that of a cat but her manner more frequently resemble those of a pretty playful kitten that be now pert and roguish now timid and demure accord to its own sweet will
her sister mary be several year old several inch tall and of a large coarser build a plain quiet sensible girl who have patiently nurse their mother through her last long tedious illness and be the housekeeper and family drudge from thence to the present time
she be trust and value by her father love and court by all dog cat child and poor people and slight and neglect by everybody else
the reverend michael millward himself be a tall ponderous elderly gentleman who place a shovel hat above his large square massive-feature face carry a stout walk-stick in his hand and incase his still powerful limb in knee-breech and gaiter or black silk stocking on state occasion
he be a man of fix principle strong prejudice and regular habit intolerant of dissent in any shape act under a firm conviction that his opinion be always right and whoever differ from they must be either most deplorably ignorant or wilfully blind
in childhood i have always be accustom to regard he with a feeling of reverential awe but lately even now surmount for though he have a fatherly kindness for the well-behave he be a strict disciplinarian and have often sternly reprove our juvenile failing and peccadillo and moreover in those day whenever he call upon our parent we have to stand up before he and say our catechism or repeat how doth the little busy bee or some other hymn or bad than all be question about his last text and the head of the discourse which we never could remember
sometimes the worthy gentleman would reprove my mother for be over-indulgent to her son with a reference to old eli or david and absalom which be particularly gall to her feeling and very highly as she respect he and all his saying i once hear her exclaim i wish to goodness he have a son himself
he wouldnot be so ready with his advice to other people then hewould see what it be to have a couple of boy to keep in order he have a laudable care for his own bodily health keep very early hour regularly take a walk before breakfast be vastly particular about warm and dry clothing have never be know to preach a sermon without previously swallow a raw egg albeit he be gift with good lung and a powerful voice and be generally extremely particular about what he eat and drank though by no mean abstemious and have a mode of dietary peculiar to himself be a great despiser of tea and such slop and a patron of malt liquor bacon and egg ham hung beef and other strong meat which agree well enough with his digestive organ and therefore be maintain by he to be good and wholesome for everybody and confidently recommend to the most delicate convalescent or dyspeptic who if they fail to derive the promise benefit from his prescription be tell it be because they have not persevere and if they complain of inconvenient result therefrom be assure it be all fancy
i will just touch upon two other person whom i have mention and then bring this long letter to a close

wilson and her daughter
the former be the widow of a substantial farmer a narrow-minded tattle old gossip whose character be not worth describing
she have two son robert a rough countrifie farmer and richard a retire studious young man who be study the classic with the vicar s assistance prepare for college with a view to enter the church
their sister jane be a young lady of some talent and more ambition

chapter ii i perceive with joy my most value friend that the cloud of your displeasure have pass away the light of your countenance bless i once more and you desire the continuation of my story therefore without more ado you shall have it
i think the day i last mention be a certain sunday the late in the october of 1827
on the following tuesday i be out with my dog and gun in pursuit of such game as i could find within the territory of linden-car but find none at all i turn my arm against the hawk and carrion crow whose depredation as i suspect have deprive i of well prey
to this end i leave the more frequented region the woode valley the corn-field and the meadow-land and proceed to mount the steep acclivity of wildfell the wild and the lofty eminence in our neighbourhood where as you ascend the hedge as well as the tree become scanty and stunt the former at length give place to rough stone fence partly green over with ivy and moss the latter to larch and scotch fir-tree or isolate blackthorn
the field be rough and stony and wholly unfit for the plough be mostly devoted to the posturing of sheep and cattle the soil be thin and poor bit of grey rock here and there peep out from the grassy hillock bilberry-plant and heather relic of more savage wildness grow under the wall and in many of the enclosure ragweed and rush usurped supremacy over the scanty herbage but these be not my property
near the top of this hill about two mile from linden-car stand wildfell hall a superannuated mansion of the elizabethan era build of dark grey stone venerable and picturesque to look at but doubtless cold and gloomy enough to inhabit with its thick stone mullion and little latticed pane its time-eat air-hole and its too lonely too unsheltered situation only shield from the war of wind and weather by a group of scotch fir themselves half blight with storm and look as stern and gloomy as the hall itself
behind it lie a few desolate field and then the brown heath-clothe summit of the hill before it enclose by stone wall and enter by an iron gate with large ball of grey granite similar to those which decorate the roof and gable surmount the gate-post be a garden once stock with such hard plant and flower as could well brook the soil and climate and such tree and shrub as could well endure the gardener s torture shear and most readily assume the shape he choose to give they now have be leave so many year untilled and untrimmed abandon to the weed and the grass to the frost and the wind the rain and the drought it present a very singular appearance indeed
the close green wall of privet that have border the principal walk be two-third wither away and the rest grow beyond all reasonable bound the old boxwood swan that sit beside the scraper have lose its neck and half its body the castellate tower of laurel in the middle of the garden the gigantic warrior that stand on one side of the gateway and the lion that guard the other be sprout into such fantastic shape as resemble nothing either in heaven or earth or in the water under the earth but to my young imagination they present all of they a goblinish appearance that harmonise well with the ghostly legion and dark tradition our old nurse have tell we respect the haunt hall and its depart occupant
i have succeed in kill a hawk and two crow when i come within sight of the mansion and then relinquish further depredation i saunter on to have a look at the old place and see what change have be work in it by its new inhabitant
i do not like to go quite to the front and stare in at the gate but i pause beside the garden wall and look and see no change except in one wing where the break window and dilapidate roof have evidently be repair and where a thin wreath of smoke be curl up from the stack of chimney
while i thus stand lean on my gun and look up at the dark gable sink in an idle reverie weave a tissue of wayward fancy in which old association and the fair young hermit now within those wall bear a nearly equal part i hear a slight rustling and scramble just within the garden and glance in the direction whence the sound proceed i behold a tiny hand elevate above the wall it cling to the topmost stone and then another little hand be raise to take a firm hold and then appear a small white forehead surmount with wreath of light brown hair with a pair of deep blue eye beneath and the upper portion of a diminutive ivory nose
the eye do not notice i but sparkle with glee on behold sancho my beautiful black and white setter that be course about the field with its muzzle to the ground
the little creature raise its face and call aloud to the dog
the good-nature animal pause look up and wag his tail but make no far advance
the child a little boy apparently about five year old scramble up to the top of the wall and call again and again but find this of no avail apparently make up his mind like mahomet to go to the mountain since the mountain would not come to he and attempt to get over but a crab old cherry- tree that grow hard by catch he by the frock in one of its crooked scraggy arm that stretch over the wall
in attempt to disengage himself his foot slip and down he tumble but not to the earth the tree still keep he suspend

in the fourth place this table must be spread for somebody and as solomon say who can eat or who else can hasten hereunto more than i and finally with your leave iwill sit down and satisfy my craving of to-day and leave to-morrow to shift for itself who know but what i may secure both this and that but you be not require to abstain from the substantial dinner of to-day you be only advise to partake of these coarser viand in such moderation as not to incapacitate you from enjoy the choicer banquet of to-morrow

arthur be get tired not of i i trust but of the idle quiet life he lead and no wonder for he have so few source of amusement he never read anything but newspaper and sporting magazine and when he see i occupy with a book he willnot let i rest till i close it

all the sweet summer be pass away without one breath of pleasure to i or benefit to he
and i have all along be look forward to this season with the fond delusive hope that we should enjoy it so sweetly together and that with god s help and my exertion it would be the mean of elevate his mind and refine his taste to a due appreciation of the salutary and pure delight of nature and peace and holy love
but now at evening when i see the round red sun sink quietly down behind those woody hill leave they sleep in a warm red golden haze i only think another lovely day be lose to he and i and at morning when rouse by the flutter and chirp of the sparrow and the gleeful twitter of the swallow all intent upon feed their young and full of life and joy in their own little frame i open the window to inhale the balmy soul-revive air and look out upon the lovely landscape laugh in dew and sunshine i too often shame that glorious scene with tear of thankless misery because he cannot feel its freshen influence and when i wander in the ancient wood and meet the little wild flower smile in my path or sit in the shadow of our noble ash-tree by the water-side with their branch gently sway in the light summer breeze that murmur through their feathery foliage my ear full of that low music mingle with the dreamy hum of insect my eye abstractedly gaze on the glassy surface of the little lake before i with the tree that crowd about its bank some gracefully bend to kiss its water some rear their stately head high above but stretch their wide arm over its margin all faithfully mirror far far down in its glassy depth though sometimes the image be partially break by the sport of aquatic insect and sometimes for a moment the whole be shiver into trembling fragment by a transient breeze that sweep the surface too roughly still i have no pleasure for the great the happiness that nature set before i the more i lament that he be not here to taste it the great the bliss we might enjoy together the more i feel our present wretchedness apart yes our he must be wretche though he may not know it and the more my sens be pleased the more my heart be oppress for he keep it with he confine amid the dust and smoke of london perhaps shut up within the wall of his own abominable club
but most of all at night when i enter my lonely chamber and look out upon the summer moon sweet regent of the sky float above i in the black blue vault of heaven shed a flood of silver radiance over park and wood and water so pure so peaceful so divine and think where be he now
what be he do at this moment
wholly unconscious of this heavenly scene perhaps revel with his boon companion perhaps god help i it be too too much

thank heaven he be come at last
but how alter
flushed and feverish listless and languid his beauty strangely diminish his vigour and vivacity quite depart

he say he be glad to be home again and god know how glad i be to get he back even as he be

i write his letter for he and get he everything he want and sometimes i read to he and sometimes i talk and sometimes only sit by he and soothe he with silent caress

i will shame he into virtue if i can and i will never let he leave i again
he be pleased with my attention it may be grateful for they
he like to have i near he and though he be peevish and testy with his servant and his dog he be gentle and kind to i
what he would be if i do not so watchfully anticipate his want and so carefully avoid or immediately desist from do anything that have a tendency to irritate or disturb he with however little reason i cannot tell
how intensely i wish he be worthy of all this care

august 24 th
arthur be himself again as lusty and reckless as light of heart and head as ever and as restless and hard to amuse as a spoilt child and almost as full of mischief too especially when wet weather keep he within door
i wish he have something to do some useful trade or profession or employment anything to occupy his head or his hand for a few hour a day and give he something besides his own pleasure to think about
if he would play the country gentleman and attend to the farm but that he know nothing about and willnot give his mind to consider or if he would take up with some literary study or learn to draw or to play as he be so fond of music i often try to persuade he to learn the piano but he be far too idle for such an undertaking he have no more idea of exert himself to overcome obstacle than he have of restrain his natural appetite and these two thing be the ruin of he
i lie they both to the charge of his harsh yet careless father and his madly indulgent mother
if ever i be a mother i will zealously strive against this crime of over- indulgence
i can hardly give it a milder name when i think of the evil it bring
happily it will soon be the shoot season and then if the weather permit he will find occupation enough in the pursuit and destruction of the partridge and pheasant we have no grouse or he might have be similarly occupy at this moment instead of lie under the acacia-tree pull poor dash s ear
but he say it be dull work shooting alone he must have a friend or two to help he

23 rd
our guest arrive about three week ago
lord and lady lowborough have now be marry above eight month and i will do the lady the credit to say that her husband be quite an altered man his look his spirit and his temper be all perceptibly change for the well since i last see he
but there be room for improvement still
he be not always cheerful nor always content and she often complain of his ill-humour which however of all person she ought to be the last to accuse he of as he never display it against she except for such conduct as would provoke a saint
he adore she still and would go to the world s end to please she
she know her power and she use it too but well know that to wheedle and coax be safe than to command she judiciously temper her despotism with flattery and blandishment enough to make he deem himself a favoured and a happy man
but she have a way of torment he in which i be a fellow- sufferer or might be if i choose to regard myself as such
this be by openly but not too glaringly coquet with mr
huntingdon who be quite willing to be her partner in the game but i donot care for it because with he i know there be nothing but personal vanity and a mischievous desire to excite my jealousy and perhaps to torment his friend and she no doubt be actuate by much the same motif only there be more of malice and less of playfulness in her manoeuvre
it be obviously therefore my interest to disappoint they both as far as i be concern by preserve a cheerful undisturbed serenity throughout and accordingly i endeavour to show the full confidence in my husband and the great indifference to the art of my attractive guest
i have never reproach the former but once and that be for laugh at lord lowborough s depressed and anxious countenance one evening when they have both be particularly provoking and then indeed i say a good deal on the subject and rebuke he sternly enough but he only laugh and say you can feel for he helen cannot you i can feel for anyone that be unjustly treat i reply and i can feel for those that injure they too why helen you be as jealous as he be cry he laugh still more and i find it impossible to convince he of his mistake
so from that time i have carefully refrain from any notice of the subject whatever and leave lord lowborough to take care of himself
he either have not the sense or the power to follow my example though he do try to conceal his uneasiness as well as he can but still it will appear in his face and his ill-humour will peep out at interval though not in the expression of open resentment they never go far enough for that
but i confess i do feel jealous at time most painfully bitterly so when she sing and play to he and he hang over the instrument and dwell upon her voice with no affected interest for then i know he be really delighted and i have no power to awaken similar fervour
i can amuse and please he with my simple song but not delight he thus

i donot like mrs

she grind her dependent pinch her servant and deprive even her daughter and herself of the real comfort of life because she will not consent to yield the palm in outward show to those who have three time her wealth and above all because she be determined she cherish son shall be enable to hold up his head with the high gentleman in the land this same son i imagine be a man of expensive habit no reckless spendthrift and no abandon sensualist but one who like to have everything handsome about he and to go to a certain length in youthful indulgence not so much to gratify his own taste as to maintain his reputation as a man of fashion in the world and a respectable fellow among his own lawless companion while he be too selfish to consider how many comfort might be obtain for his fond mother and sister with the money he thus waste upon himself as long as they can contrive to make a respectable appearance once a year when they come to town he give himself little concern about their private stinting and struggle at home
this be a harsh judgment to form of dear noble-minded generous-hearted walter but i fear it be too just
mrs
hargrave s anxiety to make good match for her daughter be partly the cause and partly the result of these error by make a figure in the world and show they off to advantage she hop to obtain well chance for they and by thus live beyond her legitimate mean and lavish so much on their brother she render they portionless and make they burden on her hand
poor milicent i fear have already fall a sacrifice to the manoeuvring of this mistaken mother who congratulate herself on have so satisfactorily discharge her maternal duty and hop to do as well for esther
but esther be a child as yet a little merry romp of fourteen as honest-hearted and as guileless and simple as her sister but with a fearless spirit of her own that i fancy her mother will find some difficulty in bend to her purpose

25 th 1823
another year be go
my little arthur live and thrive
he be healthy but not robust full of gentle playfulness and vivacity already affectionate and susceptible of passion and emotion it will be long ere he can find word to express
he have win his father s heart at last and now my constant terror be lest he should be ruin by that father s thoughtless indulgence

i love he still and he love i in his own way but oh how different from the love i could have give and once have hop to receive
how little real sympathy there exist between we how many of my thought and feeling be gloomily cloister within my own mind how much of my high and well self be indeed unmarried doom either to harden and sour in the sunless shade of solitude or to quite degenerate and fall away for lack of nutriment in this unwholesome soil
but i repeat i have no right to complain only let i state the truth some of the truth at least and see hereafter if any dark truth will blot these page
we have now be full two year unite the romance of our attachment must be wear away

and if so i can bear it well as well at least as i have bear it hitherto
arthur be not what be commonly call a bad man he have many good quality but he be a man without self-restraint or lofty aspiration a lover of pleasure give up to animal enjoyment he be not a bad husband but his notion of matrimonial duty and comfort be not my notion
judge from appearance his idea of a wife be a thing to love one devotedly and to stay at home to wait upon her husband and amuse he and minister to his comfort in every possible way while he choose to stay with she and when he be absent to attend to his interest domestic or otherwise and patiently wait his return no matter how he may be occupy in the meantime

chapter xxix those be four miserable month alternate between intense anxiety despair and indignation pity for he and pity for myself
and yet through all i be not wholly comfortless i have my darling sinless inoffensive little one to console i but even this consolation be embitter by the constantly-recur thought how shall i teach he hereafter to respect his father and yet to avoid his example but i remember that i have bring all these affliction in a manner wilfully upon myself and i determine to bear they without a murmur
at the same time i resolve not to give myself up to misery for the transgression of another and endeavour to divert myself as much as i could and besides the companionship of my child and my dear faithful rachel who evidently guess my sorrow and feel for they though she be too discreet to allude to they i have my book and pencil my domestic affair and the welfare and comfort of arthur s poor tenant and labourer to attend to and i sometimes seek and obtain amusement in the company of my young friend esther hargrave occasionally i ride over to see she and once or twice i have she to spend the day with i at the manor

hargrave do not visit london that season have no daughter to marry she think it as well to stay at home and economise and for a wonder walter come down to join she in the beginning of june and stay till near the close of august

hargrave

chapter xliv october 24 th
thank heaven i be free and safe at last
early we rise swiftly and quietly dress slowly and stealthily descend to the hall where benson stand ready with a light to open the door and fasten it after we
we be oblige to let one man into our secret on account of the box c

i slip two guineas into his hand by way of remembrance as he stand in the doorway hold the candle to light our departure with a tear in his honest grey eye and a host of good wish depict on his solemn countenance
alas

all be dark and still no light glimmer in the window no wreath of smoke obscure the star that sparkle above it in the frosty sky

we have leave grassdale many mile behind we before the round red sun arise to welcome our deliverance and if any inhabitant of its vicinity have chance to see we then as we bowl along on the top of the coach i scarcely think they would have suspect our identity
as i intend to be take for a widow i think it advisable to enter my new abode in mourning i be therefore attire in a plain black silk dress and mantle a black veil which i keep carefully over my face for the first twenty or thirty mile of the journey and a black silk bonnet which i have be constrain to borrow of rachel for want of such an article myself

that lady s cool distant manner rather chill i at first but i do my good to propitiate she and not entirely without success i think even in that first short visit for when i talk cheerfully to she she gradually become more kind and cordial and when i depart she bid i a gracious adieu hop ere long to have the pleasure of see i again
but you must not go till you have see the conservatory my aunt s winter garden say helen as i advance to take leave of she with as much philosophy and self-command as i could summon to my aid
i gladly avail myself of such a respite and follow she into a large and beautiful conservatory plentifully furnish with flower consider the season but of course i have little attention to spare for they
it be not however for any tender colloquy that my companion have bring i there my aunt be particularly fond of flower she observe and she be fond of staningley too i bring you here to offer a petition in her behalf that this may be her home as long as she live and if it be not our home likewise that i may often see she and be with she for i fear she will be sorry to lose i and though she lead a retired and contemplative life she be apt to get low- spirited if leave too much alone by all mean dear helen
do what you will with your own
i should not dream of wish your aunt to leave the place under any circumstance and we will live either here or elsewhere as you and she may determine and you shall see she as often as you like
i know she must be pain to part with you and i be willing to make any reparation in my power
i love she for your sake and her happiness shall be as dear to i as that of my own mother thank you darling
you shall have a kiss for that
good-by
there now there gilbert let i go here s arthur donot astonish his infantile brain with your madness but it be time to bring my narrative to a close
any one but you would say i have make it too long already
but for your satisfaction i will add a few word more because i know you will have a fellow-feeling for the old lady and will wish to know the last of her history
i do come again in spring and agreeably to helen s injunction do my good to cultivate her acquaintance
she receive i very kindly have be doubtless already prepare to think highly of my character by her niece s too favourable report
i turn my good side out of course and we get along marvellously well together
when my ambitious intention be make known to she she take it more sensibly than i have venture to hope
her only remark on the subject in my hearing be and so mr
markham you be go to rob i of my niece i understand
well
i hope god will prosper your union and make my dear girl happy at last
could she have be content to remain single i own i should have be well satisfied but if she must marry again i know of no one now live and of a suitable age to whom i would more willingly resign she than yourself or who would be more likely to appreciate her worth and make her truly happy as far as i can tell of course i be delighted with the compliment and hop to show she that she be not mistaken in her favourable judgment
i have however one request to offer continue she
it seem i be still to look on staningley as my home i wish you to make it your likewise for helen be attach to the place and to i as i be to she
there be painful association connect with grassdale which she cannot easily overcome and i shall not molest you with my company or interference here i be a very quiet person and shall keep my own apartment and attend to my own concern and only see you now and then of course i most readily consent to this and we live in the great harmony with our dear aunt until the day of her death which melancholy event take place a few year after melancholy not to herself for it come quietly upon she and she be glad to reach her journey s end but only to the few love friend and grateful dependent she leave behind
to return however to my own affair i be marry in summer on a glorious august morning
it take the whole eight month and all helen s kindness and goodness to boot to overcome my mother s prejudice against my bride-elect and to reconcile she to the idea of my leave linden grange and live so far away
yet she be gratified at her son s good fortune after all and proudly attribute it all to his own superior merit and endowment
i bequeath the farm to fergus with well hope of its prosperity than i should have have a year ago under similar circumstance for he have lately fall in love with the vicar of l- s eld daughter a lady whose superiority have rouse his latent virtue and stimulate he to the most surprising exertion not only to gain her affection and esteem and to obtain a fortune sufficient to aspire to her hand but to render himself worthy of she in his own eye as well as in those of her parent and in the end he be successful as you already know
as for myself i need not tell you how happily my helen and i have live together and how bless we still be in each other s society and in the promising young scion that be grow up about we
we be just now look forward to the advent of you and rose for the time of your annual visit draw nigh when you must leave your dusty smoky noisy toil strive city for a season of invigorate relaxation and social retirement with we
till then farewell gilbert markham
staningley june 10 th 1847
